  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ab37bbf9-acca-48af-959a-6db6b5a07d88'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initializeSiwa();

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_479961274" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/479961274" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/479961274?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=fiction-20th-century">fiction-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=class">class</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=chicago">chicago</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer479961274" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7662575184577667372">I have read a few of the Chicagoan Nelson Algren texts--most notably the bleakly beautiful Never Come Morning--but I had read this novel for which he received his most recognition, which also received the first National Book Award in 1950. It is basically a portrait of the underside of Chicago that he knew and loved well, the world of booze and short cons and heroin and coke addiction, of gambling and brothels and bars and baseball.<br/><br/>The main character referred to in the title is Francis Majcinek,</span>
  <span id="freeText7662575184577667372" style="display:none">I have read a few of the Chicagoan Nelson Algren texts--most notably the bleakly beautiful Never Come Morning--but I had read this novel for which he received his most recognition, which also received the first National Book Award in 1950. It is basically a portrait of the underside of Chicago that he knew and loved well, the world of booze and short cons and heroin and coke addiction, of gambling and brothels and bars and baseball.<br /><br />The main character referred to in the title is Francis Majcinek, known as "Frankie Machine,” a card dealer and drummer with the “golden arm” that also was a place where he injected morphine. He got addicted in WWII to this painkiller as his leg was filled with shrapnel. <br /><br />“He was falling between glacial walls, he didn't know how anyone could fall so far away from everyone else in the world. So far to fall, so cold all the way, so steep and dark between those morphine-coloured walls...”<br /><br />The story takes place in postwar downtown Chicago, 1946-48, after which Algren returned from the war, intending to write a war novel. What do we know about this period? Baby-making (baby boomers got their start then), middle class whie picket fences and economic renewal, in the post war happy-days-are -here-again. But as Algren makes clear, this city’s underclass was still very much struggling.<br /><br />Frankie is married to Sophie, who is wheel-chair bound after a drunken accident caused by Frankie, though some of her struggle with walking could be psychological. Frankie’s sidekick in crime is Sparrow, a thief, who is his accomplice when he accidentally kills a guy. Frankie also has an affair with Molly, when things head even more “south.” The novel ends with a poem, an ode to Frankie. That’s a kind of quick summary of what happens.<br /><br />The main focus of the book is Chicago’s down and out, sprinkled with contemporary music and a sort of gutter lyricism that shows his sympathy and passion for the people he meets on the streets. The dialogue is pitch perfect, showing a range of ethnicities in a variety of places from bars to strip clubs to jail to cramped apartments. <br /><br /><br />Hemingway said of the book, “Algren can hit with both hands and move around and he will kill you if you are not awfully careful. . . Mr. Algren, boy, you are good.”<br /><br />Tough guy, muscular poetry that favors the lost, the gone, the disenfranchised. <br /><br />This is what Hem means:<br /><br />“If Jesus Christ treated me like you do, I’d drive in the nails myself.”<br /><br />“For way down there, in a shot glass's false bottom, everything was bound to turn out fine after all.”<br /><br />“I couldn't buy the lice off a sick cat," the cabbie answered from the very depths of self-deprecation.”<br /><br />“Then the wooden benches along the walls, where so many outcasts had slept, would be lit by a sort of slow, clocked lightning til the bulb steadied and fastened its tiny feral fury upon the center of the room like a single sullen and manic eye. To burn on there with a steady hate. Til morning wearied and dimmed it away to nothing more than some sort of little old lost gray child of a district-station moon, all its hatred spent.”<br /><br />Leonard Cohen’s “Stranger Song,” which features some of the novel in it:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZqlR5D0y23s">https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZqlR5...</a><br /><br />"O you've seen that man before / his golden arm dispatching cards / but now it's rusted from the elbows to the finger."<br /><br />I listened to a 17-minute reading by Algren himself on Hoopla of a short version of Frankie and Sophie’s story.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7662575184577667372" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_216174350" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/216174350" />
    <a title="Drew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4675538-drew"><img alt="Drew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299606167p2/4675538.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/216174350?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drew" href="/user/show/4675538-drew">Drew</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4675538-drew?shelf=national-book-award">national-book-award</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer216174350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10874756635194551140">Listen up, those of you who loved <i>Hard Rain Falling</i>. Carpenter's good, but as far as I can tell from just reading one book from each of them, Carpenter owes just about everything he's got to Algren.<br/><br/><i>The Man With the Golden Arm</i> follows Frankie Machine, morphine-addict and sometime card-dealer, on a slow path of dissolution--my favorite kind of path. It's similar to <i>Infinite Jest</i> in its sober and sobering study of addiction and the cycle of poverty, and I have a hard time believing Sergeant McGanti</span>
  <span id="freeText10874756635194551140" style="display:none">Listen up, those of you who loved <i>Hard Rain Falling</i>. Carpenter's good, but as far as I can tell from just reading one book from each of them, Carpenter owes just about everything he's got to Algren.<br /><br /><i>The Man With the Golden Arm</i> follows Frankie Machine, morphine-addict and sometime card-dealer, on a slow path of dissolution--my favorite kind of path. It's similar to <i>Infinite Jest</i> in its sober and sobering study of addiction and the cycle of poverty, and I have a hard time believing Sergeant McGantic wasn't the direct inspiration for Wallace's smiley-faced Sergeant-at-Arms.<br /><br />Not that that's the only angle, of course. Frankie falls in with a young punk called Sparrow, they become friends, and a murder causes any number of problems between them, driving the tension of the second half of the story. This was apparently the main thrust of the whole book until someone told Algren he needed another angle, so he added in the morphine stuff later. Which is shocking; I never, ever would have guessed that. Algren weaves it in so seamlessly that it seems like it was the impetus for the book all along. <br /><br />And the style! It might be a little lyrical for the tastes of some, but the effortlessness of it negates any distaste I may have had for the more purple passages. And there's a great contrast between the grittiness of the dialogue and the grace of the narration. Here's a sample quote from among the more lyrical:<br /><br />"A roach had leaped, or fallen, from the ceiling into the water bucket, where a soggy slice of pumpernickel and a sodden hunk of sausage now circled slowly, about and about, although there was no current. Belly upward, the roach's legs plied the alien air, trying dreamily to regain a foothold; while Frankie, leaning dreamily on one elbow, knew just how that felt." A little facile, maybe; we already probably could have guessed that Frankie felt that way. But the phrasing is so good as to cancel that out and then some. Or this:<br /><br />"The marks of debauchery were seamed across his face like a chronic disease."<br /><br />Compare that with dialogue that's firmly colloquial and seems very believable as far as I can tell (this, by the way, from an old Polish cuckold, talking to his wife and her lover):<br /><br />"Work <i>all</i> day, seven days, no days off, buy nize t'ings by howz, pay <i>grocernia</i>, pay <i>buczernia</i>, pay mens I don't even know what's for, comes time to sleep everyt'ing all paid 'n nize clean howz so <i>ever</i>'body sleep--who comes by howz from whisky tavern? Mrs. No-good with dronk pocket-picker! Should be in bed by h<i>oo</i>sband instead on head 'n makes funny: 'Is Christmas, now we fight all night!' Is somethin' got to happen, is <i>all</i>."<br /><br />That's way more doctored, of course, than the voices of the other, less heavily-accented characters. But regarding the frequent italics: I remember taking issue with Nicola Barker's excessive use of same in <i>Darkmans</i>, but this is different. Maybe that's because I believe modern-day Brits don't place tons of emphasis on individual words within a sentence, but I very much do believe that Chicagoans from the 1940's did. And Algren picks up on it, elucidating the cadences of that speech and doing a good job of bringing me right there.<br /><br />I really don't know what else to say about this, because again, I feel like I've lost the capacity to intelligently review a book, maybe because I don't have whole afternoons to devote to it like I used to, but overall, the quality of the prose and the timelessness and profoundness of the story have me convinced that Algren is seriously underread and underrated.<br /><br />"</span>
  <a data-text-id="10874756635194551140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1857411879" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1857411879" />
    <a title="Scott Sigler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1782315-scott-sigler"><img alt="Scott Sigler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442455917p2/1782315.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1857411879?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Sigler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Sigler" href="/user/show/1782315-scott-sigler">Scott Sigler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1857411879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15475342693805246907">I'm not entirely sure how I feel about this book.<br/><br/>First off, the writing. Holy crap can Algren write. The language is lush and gorgeous. His ability to paint vivid character portraits is among the best I've ever read. Analogy and metaphor are this cat's playground. While I'm not much for the world of literature, it's easy to see why this won the National Book Award in 1950.<br/><br/>On the other hand, though, is the story itself. Goddamn depressing. Wait, I should use all-caps: IT IS GODDAMNED DEPRESSING. </span>
  <span id="freeText15475342693805246907" style="display:none">I'm not entirely sure how I feel about this book.<br /><br />First off, the writing. Holy crap can Algren write. The language is lush and gorgeous. His ability to paint vivid character portraits is among the best I've ever read. Analogy and metaphor are this cat's playground. While I'm not much for the world of literature, it's easy to see why this won the National Book Award in 1950.<br /><br />On the other hand, though, is the story itself. Goddamn depressing. Wait, I should use all-caps: IT IS GODDAMNED DEPRESSING. All of the characters a miserable, trapped in the tenements with no future, no hope, and steadily destroyed their lives with booze and drugs. There is no plot. I repeat, there is <em>no plot</em>. Like a lot of literature (which may be the reason I don't gravitate toward it), this is simply a slice of life from a rough part of Chicago. Aside from the drinking, cavorting, complaining and succumbing to base instincts, the characters don't do anything. <br /><br />So combine amazing writing, and a story without a plot, and you get my four stars. If you are a writer, read it. Period. If you are not a writer and you like stories with a plot, it's not for you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15475342693805246907" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1178397848" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1178397848" />
    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/16342516-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1358634810p2/16342516.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1178397848?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/16342516-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1178397848" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10765508493413504747">I grew up in Chicago in the neighborhood Algren writes about and at the same time he was writing about it ... so from the beginning I was at odds with this book. This isn't the neighborhood that I grew up in! But ... after finishing the book and thinking about some of my Polish relatives who either owned taverns or spent a lot of time in taverns I have to reluctantly admit that Algren is portraying a part of Chicago that I was simply too young to know about.<br/><br/>Some reviewers have referred to this n</span>
  <span id="freeText10765508493413504747" style="display:none">I grew up in Chicago in the neighborhood Algren writes about and at the same time he was writing about it ... so from the beginning I was at odds with this book. This isn't the neighborhood that I grew up in! But ... after finishing the book and thinking about some of my Polish relatives who either owned taverns or spent a lot of time in taverns I have to reluctantly admit that Algren is portraying a part of Chicago that I was simply too young to know about.<br /><br />Some reviewers have referred to this neighborhood as a slum. Well, this neighborhood at that time was most definitely not a slum. It was a blue-collar, Polish and Jewish working class neighborhood. Many of the buildings in this Wicker Park neighborhood were divided into rental apartments. These three-story buildings often had two apartments per floor and were protected by rent controls for a number of years after World War II. The streets were clean and many of the kids walked to Catholic schools. Milwaukee Avenue, often referred to as Polish Broadway, was a main shopping area with lots of nice stores. The store he calls Niebolts was Wiebolts department store and Golds was Goldblatts department store. Both are now long gone but served the working class very well.<br /><br />The kind of neighborhood taverns where people sit around and play cards, drink, and maybe even do dope can still be found in virtually any little as well as big town today. If you look for it you will find it. Algren, at the time had little money and this was an inexpensive neighborhood to live in. He seems to have been naturally attracted to this level of society. He liked to drink and gamble and he liked to write about people who for whatever reason were stuck in a world they were not likely to ever escape from. Algren portrays them as they really were -- mostly not very nice. But, they were fellow human beings and as such they were worthy of having their story told as well.<br /><br />His writing style is lyrical and poetic.....deliberately quite a contrast to the folks he is writing about. This book was hard to get through because these people are so unappealing, but I'm really glad I made the effort. I think he was rightly given the first National Book Award for this novel. In a cruel joke that I'm sure Algren appreciated he never made a penny off the movie version of his book!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10765508493413504747" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_409654334" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/409654334" />
    <a title="Andrew" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/442654-andrew"><img alt="Andrew" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1513093267p2/442654.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/409654334?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Andrew" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Andrew" href="/user/show/442654-andrew">Andrew</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/442654-andrew?shelf=american-fiction">american-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer409654334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15535265083009439151">The old American myth is that if we work hard and have a properly optimistic attitude, the world is at our fingertips. Life is good and good for you in God's Country. This is bullshit.<br/><br/>And Nelson Algren, at the height of the McCarthy Era, had the courage to say so. His are the stories of all the American dreamers who lost out. While the story drags a bit at time, it's still compelling. Algren breaks up the storyline with long, poetically gorgeous ruminations about sociology, psychology, and what </span>
  <span id="freeText15535265083009439151" style="display:none">The old American myth is that if we work hard and have a properly optimistic attitude, the world is at our fingertips. Life is good and good for you in God's Country. This is bullshit.<br /><br />And Nelson Algren, at the height of the McCarthy Era, had the courage to say so. His are the stories of all the American dreamers who lost out. While the story drags a bit at time, it's still compelling. Algren breaks up the storyline with long, poetically gorgeous ruminations about sociology, psychology, and what it is to be down and out in the most prosperous nation on the planet. The net effect is a remarkably complete-seeming and poignant picture of a forgotten hellscape of addiction, squalor, and the derailment of the immigrant dream.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15535265083009439151" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1647794118" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1647794118" />
    <a title="robin friedman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman"><img alt="robin friedman" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571353109p2/22269025.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1647794118?book_show_action=true">May 24, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="robin friedman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="robin friedman" href="/user/show/22269025-robin-friedman">robin friedman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1647794118" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17628544032110458154">A Dark Novel From The Poet Of The Lost<br/><br/>I had the opportunity to watch a new documentary film, "Nelson Algren: The End is Nothing, the Road is All" in which one of the interviewees refers to Algren (1909 -- 1981) as the "poet of the lost". The film moved me to revisit Algren, a writer I don't know well, and to read his best-known novel, "The Man With The Golden Arm". Algren received the first National Book Award for this novel in 1950 -- an outstanding initial choice for a premiere literary award.</span>
  <span id="freeText17628544032110458154" style="display:none">A Dark Novel From The Poet Of The Lost<br /><br />I had the opportunity to watch a new documentary film, "Nelson Algren: The End is Nothing, the Road is All" in which one of the interviewees refers to Algren (1909 -- 1981) as the "poet of the lost". The film moved me to revisit Algren, a writer I don't know well, and to read his best-known novel, "The Man With The Golden Arm". Algren received the first National Book Award for this novel in 1950 -- an outstanding initial choice for a premiere literary award. In the novel, Algren tells the story of lost, lonely individuals. He writes with a harsh beauty amply justifying the film's reference to him as the "poet of the lost". The novelist Kurt Vonnegut who knew Algren refers to him near the end of the film as "the loneliest man I ever met", a description that would apply to many of the characters in Algren's award-winning novel.<br /><br />The novel is set in the bars, cheap apartments, prisons, and streets frequented by the Chicago underclass in 1947- 1948. The novel's main character, who goes by the name of Frankie Machine, has acquired the nickname of the "man with the golden arm" due in part to his steadiness in dealing cards. Frankie aspires to put his steadiness of arm, wrist and hand to use by becoming a jazz drummer. Characters in this novel often are called by their roles, and Frankie is known as "Dealer". Frankie served in the Army in WW II, took a severe wound to the stomach, and became a morphine addict. Algren's novel is one of the first to explore seriously and realistically the use of drugs.<br /><br />The novel is filled with low life, highly differentiated characters, including Frankie's friend Solly, a mildly-retarded petty thief who usually is called Sparrow, or "punk". Frankie is unhappily married to Sophie, called "Zosh" who is bitter and confined to a wheelchair after an accident with Frankie driving the car. Frankie has a mistress, Mollie, a stripper and bar maid; Sparrow has a mistress, Violet, whom he sees when her husband, the Old Man is asleep or in his cups. The book is replete with shady, colorful characters, including the bar owner, the keeper of the fixed card games that uses Frankie as the dealer, crooked lawyers, quack doctors, gamblers, drunks, petty criminals, and fixers.<br /><br />The plot develops slowly and involves Frankie and Sparrow's relationship and the accidental killing of the fixer, Louis, which results in Frankie's attempt to evade the law. The novel is in two lengthy sections with most of the action and plot development taking place in the second section. Most of the book consists of a lengthy series of vignettes of varying lengths separated by paragraph breaks. These small sections each focus on a particular scene and a small group of individuals. They develop character and settings. Aspects of the story get foretold in each of the settings but dimly so with the overall focus of the story becoming clear only as it proceeds. The scenes are often not chronological and sometimes tend to run into each other with an almost surrealistic effect.<br /><br />Much of the novel is in dialogue and full of the slang of the late 1940s. The book is replete with religious, racial, and national derogatory terms that would not meet contemporary standards The book is full of quotations from billboards, ads, and popular songs. The omniscient narrator's voice is, in contrast to the dialogue, poetic and rhythmical. With its lyricism, the novel concludes fittingly with a poem. Throughout the book, the narrator describes and comments on the characters and their actions with a mix of compassion and irony. In this passage early in the novel, the narrator comments on the American dream through Frankie's eyes.<br /><br />"The great, secret and special American guilt of owning nothing, nothing at all, in the one land where ownership and virtue are one. Guilt that lay crouched behind every billboard which gave each man his commandments; for each man here had failed the billboards all down the line. No Ford in this one's future nor ever any place all his own. Had failed before the radio commercials, by the street car plugs and by the standards of every self-respecting magazine. With his own eyes he had seen the truer Americans mount the broad stone stairways to success surely and swiftly and unaided by others; he was always the one left alone, it seemed at last, without enough sense of honor to climb off a West Madison Street Keep-Our-City-Clean box and not enough ambition to raise his eyes back to the billboards."<br /><br />The following passage describes a nightly gathering of suspects in a local police station.<br /><br />" Yet they come on and come on, and where they come from no captain knows and where they go no captain goes: mush workers and lush workers, catamites and sodomites, bucket workers and bail jumpers, till tappers and assistant pickpockets, square johns and copper johns; lamisters and hallroom boys, ancient pious perverts and old blown parolees, rapoes and record-men; the damned and the undaunted, the jaunty and condemned."<br /><br />The novel starts slowly and with some rough edges gathers in force and conviction. The reader gradually gets drawn into the settings and develops a feeling for the characters and their struggles and failings without romanticizing them. "We are all members of one another" is a theme driven home in the work through all the stories of isolation, frustration and loneliness. "The Man With The Golden Arm" is slow and difficult; but it is an American masterwork. I am grateful to the documentary I saw for getting me to read this novel at last.<br /><br />Robin Friedman</span>
  <a data-text-id="17628544032110458154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13984676-lee-foust?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4713914639387239721">It's rough, it's sloppy, it's poetic, meandering, even--I think--contradictory at one point, and ultimately exquisitely sad and utterly beautiful. Particularly effecting here is the mix of cynicism with vulnerability, making this novel maybe the finest depiction of that human condition in which we're always bleeding and always finding novel ways to stem the blood flow by pretending it doesn't hurt that much, by ridiculing the pain as if we don't feel it at all.<br/><br/>My first Algren, I can't wait to re</span>
  <span id="freeText4713914639387239721" style="display:none">It's rough, it's sloppy, it's poetic, meandering, even--I think--contradictory at one point, and ultimately exquisitely sad and utterly beautiful. Particularly effecting here is the mix of cynicism with vulnerability, making this novel maybe the finest depiction of that human condition in which we're always bleeding and always finding novel ways to stem the blood flow by pretending it doesn't hurt that much, by ridiculing the pain as if we don't feel it at all.<br /><br />My first Algren, I can't wait to read another. It packs much of the same wallop of a Hubert Selby Jr., a favorite of mine, but with perhaps a less controlled narrative, and, most interestingly, without the ostensibly Christian empathy angle from which Selby created his works. So, either Algren arrived at depicting the naked, suffering human from a purely secular, existentialist angle--logical, it seems to me, as that's pretty much my own philosophical stance and I always fall back on a love of humanity, and particularly useless suffering as our/humanity's most salient characteristic and therefore the greatest beauty of our restlessly self-destructive species--or that he was a Christian beneath his writerly pose. <br /><br />Speaking of which, I enjoyed the extra essays in this critical edition, especially the personal memoir of John Clellon Holmes, who puts Algren into what we have to figure now is his historical context. As well as bemoaning how short is the shelf life of writing--shorter, sadly, than the lives of most writers. This is why I waited until I was 50 to publish. I wish.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer789328597" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3575716449929721058">The Man with the Golden Arm is an incredible peace of American literature which tells the story of Frankie Machine. When Frankie arrives home to Chicago from the Second World War he comes back with a Morphine habit which he initially hides from his associates and wife. <br/><br/>Life for Frankie is hard, he dreams of being a drummer but his real skill is in his arm, being a card dealer. He uses his skills to make his money. However back at home his wife Sophie is not making life easy for him either. She h</span>
  <span id="freeText3575716449929721058" style="display:none">The Man with the Golden Arm is an incredible peace of American literature which tells the story of Frankie Machine. When Frankie arrives home to Chicago from the Second World War he comes back with a Morphine habit which he initially hides from his associates and wife. <br /><br />Life for Frankie is hard, he dreams of being a drummer but his real skill is in his arm, being a card dealer. He uses his skills to make his money. However back at home his wife Sophie is not making life easy for him either. She has been in a wheelchair since her and Frankie had a car accident however it is suggested the illness is psychosomatic- she certainly uses this to keep Frankie with her. As the book progresses life for Frankie becomes more and more unbearable and his habit spirals out of control.<br /><br />I had a really hard time grading this book because I found it a really difficult read, it took me a long time to get into it and although there were some very interesting sections I found it hard going. However the contribution Nelson Algren made to the history of literature with this book cannot be ignored. It was one of the first novels which saw poorer classes being depicted as poor and whats more not trying to better themselves, unlike authors before him Algren was trying to tell his readers that sometimes when society thinks people are bad they really are. The excellence of the writing and the quality story cannot be ignored and I would encourage others to read this novel. It truly deserves it's place on the 1001 books to Read Before you Die list. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3575716449929721058" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText14511077509978616389" style="display:none">A mind-blowing book, set in the tenements and bars of the down-and-out in pre-WWII Chicago. the main character a junkie card-dealer whose arm is golden because of his steady dealing skills and the lines of scars. an amazing mixture of idiomatic language capturing the thoughts, ticks, and dreams of the homeless, alcoholics, cripples, bar owners and prostitutes and a pristine, lyrical narrative voice- you are both in the world and looking in into its tragedy. is this book christian in its negative utopia?-- redemption through utter despair, failure, and heart-wrenching betrayals-- or is it a negative image of a communist ideal? </span>

<div id="review_2978460465" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Josh Friedlander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7226831-josh-friedlander"><img alt="Josh Friedlander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1498135749p2/7226831.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Josh Friedlander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Josh Friedlander" href="/user/show/7226831-josh-friedlander">Josh Friedlander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781841955612" href="/book/show/350871.The_Man_with_the_Golden_Arm">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=existentialist">existentialist</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7226831-josh-friedlander?shelf=noir">noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2978460465" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11380812843768803780">A bleak tale of wretched lives in post-WWII Polish Chicago, in the shadow of drug abuse (which drug is never exactly stated, heroin maybe?) The writing occasionally becomes mawkish, bringing to mind Oscar Wilde's line about Little Dorritt. All the characters live beyond hope or growth, neglected and just hoping to get by. You might read this as social critique, but Algren is mostly content to just wallow in the pathos of it all. It does not, of course, end well. The writing is stirring in parts,</span>
  <span id="freeText11380812843768803780" style="display:none">A bleak tale of wretched lives in post-WWII Polish Chicago, in the shadow of drug abuse (which drug is never exactly stated, heroin maybe?) The writing occasionally becomes mawkish, bringing to mind Oscar Wilde's line about Little Dorritt. All the characters live beyond hope or growth, neglected and just hoping to get by. You might read this as social critique, but Algren is mostly content to just wallow in the pathos of it all. It does not, of course, end well. The writing is stirring in parts, and the plot gets better as it progresses, but it is overall a bit too long. There is a film with Frank Sinatra and Kim Novak with a significantly altered plot, which Algren disowned. Noir and literary portrayals of inner-city lowlifes owe a debt to this book: it's hard to imagine the <i>New Yorker</i> of today complaining, as it did then, about the idea "that we live in a society whose bums and tramps are better men than the preachers and the politicians and the otherwise respectables." (Algren led an interesting life, dating Simone de Beauvoir, being blacklisted and vanishing from literary life. There is a good recent NYer profile <a href="https://www.newyorker.com/magazine/2019/04/15/nelson-algrens-street-cred" rel="nofollow noopener">here</a>.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="11380812843768803780" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer61528021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9502331407238629462">I have had a beautiful (if a tad yellow around the edges) used copy of "The Man with the Golden Arm" sitting on my shelf, unread, for 5 years now. Part of me did not want to read it for fear of damaging its aging cover by hauling the book to and fro, and part of me was immensely turned off by his other writings. Algren has a tendency to romanticize too much, to assign higher meanings to low functioning people existing in a sub-prime city. <br/><br/>"The Man...," however, hits on so many universal truths, </span>
  <span id="freeText9502331407238629462" style="display:none">I have had a beautiful (if a tad yellow around the edges) used copy of "The Man with the Golden Arm" sitting on my shelf, unread, for 5 years now. Part of me did not want to read it for fear of damaging its aging cover by hauling the book to and fro, and part of me was immensely turned off by his other writings. Algren has a tendency to romanticize too much, to assign higher meanings to low functioning people existing in a sub-prime city. <br /><br />"The Man...," however, hits on so many universal truths, and does so using such poetical precision that the romanticization of the gritty works. Algren's writing is truly flawless, from a stylistic standpoint. There is not a wasted line in the entire novel. It is a wonder that this book is not talked about more, as it should probably be somewhere in the canon of Great American Literature, and I may need to take another look at the rest of Algren's catalogue...</span>
  <a data-text-id="9502331407238629462" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_56443466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56443466" />
    <a title="Tim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1486850-tim"><img alt="Tim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220403323p2/1486850.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56443466?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tim" href="/user/show/1486850-tim">Tim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781888363180" href="/book/show/840507.The_Man_with_the_Golden_Arm">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56443466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15267716586728394484">I think I want to like this book more than I did. If I had read it ten years ago, I think I would have thought it was completely incredible, but I've spent a lot of time reading a bunch of Bukowski and listening to a bunch of Tom Waits and the 'hard times' theme is so familiar that it's difficult to fully appreciate that this guy was doing it long before the stuff I've mostly read. Either way, this is a pretty awesome Chicago novel, and Algren knows how to hit hard with his phrasing. He has a ni</span>
  <span id="freeText15267716586728394484" style="display:none">I think I want to like this book more than I did. If I had read it ten years ago, I think I would have thought it was completely incredible, but I've spent a lot of time reading a bunch of Bukowski and listening to a bunch of Tom Waits and the 'hard times' theme is so familiar that it's difficult to fully appreciate that this guy was doing it long before the stuff I've mostly read. Either way, this is a pretty awesome Chicago novel, and Algren knows how to hit hard with his phrasing. He has a nice combination of American tell-it-like-it-is style and a more Euro "I read a lot of Henry Miller and liked it" style, so I dug that. Also, a brutal rendition of morphine addiction. Algren keeps the preaching for the most part out, which is nice, but it's pretty clear his symphathies are leftist. <br />I guess I actually want to give this a 3.5 or something. I want to give it a 4 but something about it bored me at parts and I kept putting it down for long stretches and reading other things inbetween. Also, a bunch of bands reference this book, which I thought was funny and didn't really realize going into it that that was the case. But you'll catch references from Dillinger 4 songs and I think the Hold Steady too? Maybe that pre-hold steady band, I don't know. <br />The characters are pretty awesome- at least the ones that are developed. There are a lot of placeholder characters who just exist to perform one or two actions, or as kinda everyman characters from skid row, but the main characters- Frankie Machine, Solly Saltskin, Zosh, Molly Novotny.... really incredible depth of character.<br /><br /><br />anyway, read it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15267716586728394484" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11703900085859935493">Some cats just swing that way...<br/><br/>The book is so good I don't know what to say about it but I can say something about that introduction because that was plain awful. Again and again Giles tells us how Algren "challenges" us to identify with these grotesque poor people. Well when I first started reading this I did have that wow moment of damn, I never did quite imagine so clearly what it would be like to actually be one of those putrid crusty drunks leering cock-eyed from a bar stool (I'm thinking </span>
  <span id="freeText11703900085859935493" style="display:none">Some cats just swing that way...<br /><br />The book is so good I don't know what to say about it but I can say something about that introduction because that was plain awful. Again and again Giles tells us how Algren "challenges" us to identify with these grotesque poor people. Well when I first started reading this I did have that wow moment of damn, I never did quite imagine so clearly what it would be like to actually be one of those putrid crusty drunks leering cock-eyed from a bar stool (I'm thinking of Blind Pig here...) But by the time I ended it I wasn't thinking about social justice or middle class privilege or how proud of myself I was that I had identified with the characters. I was just - in awe. Of some of the most seductive writing and tragic characters I have ever met. Sophie stuck in her wheelchair by the force of her own spite, watching out the window as she waits for Frankie to come home. The "piece of trade" downstairs, waiting too, as the el billows her white curtains. Record Head Bedmar mulling and mulling on how we are all members of one another. All members of one another. The punk scampering along behind his idol. Violet dragging her velvet train around picking up cigarette butts telling all the men at the party that they can only kiss her if they compliment her husband's socks. And Frankie Machine of course running and running from that monkey but the monkey keeps on laughing at him just the same. All the riffraff of West Division street come together to make this one of the best tributes humanity's got.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11703900085859935493" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <a title="Deodand" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/264971-deodand"><img alt="Deodand" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186628326p2/264971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/29354027?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deodand" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deodand" href="/user/show/264971-deodand">Deodand</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/264971-deodand?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/264971-deodand?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29354027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11606984010331358777">This book is a powerful...well, everything. There is great power in the story, such that you will fall down a dark hole. I could read a book like this at a rock concert and not hear a note. The world is so fully realized that, while I was reading, whatever was going on around me disappeared. I was there!<br/><br/>There is a strong indictment against poverty, but the stronger message was against alcohol. Algren's written a time capsule for those of us born after the negative effects of drink were well-know</span>
  <span id="freeText11606984010331358777" style="display:none">This book is a powerful...well, everything. There is great power in the story, such that you will fall down a dark hole. I could read a book like this at a rock concert and not hear a note. The world is so fully realized that, while I was reading, whatever was going on around me disappeared. I was there!<br /><br />There is a strong indictment against poverty, but the stronger message was against alcohol. Algren's written a time capsule for those of us born after the negative effects of drink were well-known. Nearly every character in the book is soaked in booze. They always said it was the opiate of the masses, didn't they? I was also fascinated by how the cops hauled someone off the streets for any reason. When was the last time you heard of anyone going to jail for loitering? Or cursing?<br /><br />Algren's heart is clearly with his characters, but he doesn't sugarcoat an inch of this book. The female characters are every bit as corrupt as the males. He also wrote the story in the third person omniscient - and boy does he mean omniscient. We get to read the thoughts of the dogs and cats.<br /><br />Keep an eye out for his mastery of alliteration and the excellent use of lists.<br /><br />I can certainly see why this book inspired a raft of songwriters, poets and other novelists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11606984010331358777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer24323783" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17771067862099208834">I can't believe I've put off reading Algren for so long. I had no idea what a brilliant writer he was. Not to mention, as a born and raised Chicagoan, 3rd generation Pole, and having lived in Ukrainian Village for a spell, I have a special appreciation for his descriptions of post WWII Chicago and his masterful use of the vernacular. The image of Piggy-O's bleeding gums turning the froth of his beer pink is one I can't erase from my mind and I love the quote about "the great, secret and special </span>
  <span id="freeText17771067862099208834" style="display:none">I can't believe I've put off reading Algren for so long. I had no idea what a brilliant writer he was. Not to mention, as a born and raised Chicagoan, 3rd generation Pole, and having lived in Ukrainian Village for a spell, I have a special appreciation for his descriptions of post WWII Chicago and his masterful use of the vernacular. The image of Piggy-O's bleeding gums turning the froth of his beer pink is one I can't erase from my mind and I love the quote about "the great, secret and special American guilt of owning nothing, nothing at all in the one land where ownership and virtue are one." Sparrow Saltskin is without a doubt one of the most memorable characters ever written, and the ever-present rumbling of the "el" conjures up so many ghosts of various apartments I lived in as a child and as an adult where the train provided the soundtrack for my life. This book is achingly sad, funny and beautiful. A brilliant tribute to all the untold stories and unsung lives from my grandparents' generation and on. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17771067862099208834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1841858564" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1841858564" />
    <a title="Alex Laser" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5776055-alex-laser"><img alt="Alex Laser" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554604589p2/5776055.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1841858564?book_show_action=true">Jan 03, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex Laser" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex Laser" href="/user/show/5776055-alex-laser">Alex Laser</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1841858564" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2747967829046458140">A beautiful elegy to the down-and-out. Algren's work lies somewhere on the spectrum between Ashcan naturalism of the early 1900s and the hyperrealism of the 1980. His ability to lend dimension and patronizing-free empathy to the hustlers and con artists of West Side Chicago, while inverting cops from would-be saviors into wretched guilt-ridden philosophers, makes this book a forerunner to works as diverse in genre as the literary nonficiton "In Cold Blood", the songs of Lou Reed & The Velvet Und</span>
  <span id="freeText2747967829046458140" style="display:none">A beautiful elegy to the down-and-out. Algren's work lies somewhere on the spectrum between Ashcan naturalism of the early 1900s and the hyperrealism of the 1980. His ability to lend dimension and patronizing-free empathy to the hustlers and con artists of West Side Chicago, while inverting cops from would-be saviors into wretched guilt-ridden philosophers, makes this book a forerunner to works as diverse in genre as the literary nonficiton "In Cold Blood", the songs of Lou Reed &amp; The Velvet Underground, and the television drama "The Wire". Most of all, beyond the linguistic talents, this book is just a damn good story, complete with plot twists, hooks and humor. Algren blends midwestern simplicity and sensible storytelling with big city majesty and ambition. The book roars with the audacity of the "El" train looping dirty skyscrapers and illuminated dive bars indiscriminately. This is a true Chicago work through and through.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2747967829046458140" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9997081379382849873">I recently read Ironweed by William Kennedy, and found similarities between The Man with the Golden Arm and Ironweed. Both deal with individuals who begin with poor prospects in life and through a series of choices, missteps and bad luck progress into a downward spiral with no real chance of breaking away from their lifestyle.   <span id="freeText9997081379382849873" style="display:none">I recently read Ironweed by William Kennedy, and found similarities between The Man with the Golden Arm and Ironweed. Both deal with individuals who begin with poor prospects in life and through a series of choices, missteps and bad luck progress into a downward spiral with no real chance of breaking away from their lifestyle. Sound depressing? It can be. The Man with the Golden Arm was extremely well written, but I enjoyed Ironweed more. I struggled though as to why. Francis Phelan in Ironweed has a chance at a different life, but there is an acceptance on his part of the life that he had come to know. Did that somehow make me feel better about Francis Phelan in Ironweed as compared to Frankie Machine in The Man with the Golden Arm. I hope not. Francis was a more likable character than Frankie. Francis cared about those around him and tried to help them. Maybe that was the difference. But that might just mean the story of Frankie Machine was more realistic.</span>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2404372086" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2404372086" />
    <a title="René" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5044802-ren"><img alt="René" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576389323p2/5044802.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2404372086?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="René" class="user" itemprop="url" name="René" href="/user/show/5044802-ren">René</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2404372086" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17385349554446515192">I enjoyed this despite the ultra-bleak setting and characters. The story takes place in post-WWII Chicago in a working-class, Polish immigrant neighborhood (Division and Milwaukee), and the characters are all drunks and bums, gamblers, thieves, prostitutes, panhandlers, bartenders, janitors, newsies, delivery people, war veterans, draft flunkies, junkies, drug dealers, spiteful wives, two-timing women, prison wardens, and cops. Also a couple dogs (one of which is also a lush), cats (one of which</span>
  <span id="freeText17385349554446515192" style="display:none">I enjoyed this despite the ultra-bleak setting and characters. The story takes place in post-WWII Chicago in a working-class, Polish immigrant neighborhood (Division and Milwaukee), and the characters are all drunks and bums, gamblers, thieves, prostitutes, panhandlers, bartenders, janitors, newsies, delivery people, war veterans, draft flunkies, junkies, drug dealers, spiteful wives, two-timing women, prison wardens, and cops. Also a couple dogs (one of which is also a lush), cats (one of which can predict when people are gonna die), and wagon horses. The narrative is a solid combo of working-class Chicago dialect and Algren's gritty, distinctive, slyly complex prose. Some passages read like poetry, sometimes Algren's writing verges on purplish, often it features humor and beauty nevermind the setting, and always the undercurrent is sheer rage. Because of its bleakness and anger, it isn't an easy book to get through -- I can see why some readers would give up, especially non-American readers who aren't used to reading American dialects. Still, I found it really beautiful and readable. I may be biased given I was born in Chicago and spent many years living and working there, and my parents, siblings, one grandparent, and one or two great-grandparents were also born and raised there. I never lived in the neighborhood where this book is set, but it makes little difference, as there are similar neighborhoods scattered throughout the city and even in the suburbs, if you can see your way past all the Starbucks, vape shops, faux-Irish pubs, and boutique dog biscuit bakeries and hipster babywear stores. While gentrification has been working overtime for decades in Algren's old stomping grounds and all around the city's north and west sides and the Loop to flush out all the classic unsavory Algrenesque characters and rip down their homes and haunts in favor of a million and more condos, coffee shops, and big-box shopping complexes, if you ride the city's buses and el trains at all or hang around the tiny old neighborhood bars and corner taverns that populate still undiscovered (by hipsters and yuppies) areas like Portage Park or Dunning (where I was born), you can easily find the same old cast of misfits and mess-ups that makes "The Man With the Golden Arm" ring so sad, true, and heartbreaking.<br /><br />I took away one star from my rating because there were times when Algren just seemed to go on past his point, where I sensed he was a little overly in love with his own writing. Also, I found the gambling/poker scenes a bit hard to follow. Some of the essays that follow the novel in the 50th anniversary edition are quite interesting. Those would've benefited from another essay or two by a female writer (there's only one included, by Algren biographer Bettina Drew), or someone who might have provided a closer look at Algren's machismo and his era's derisive view of women (in particular, of women who write) and whether that had an effect on this novel's gender politics. (At times, Algren writes with incredible empathy for women, despite his real-life womanizing and macho interests.) I think the one thing this edition really could've used was an excerpt from Simone de Beauvoir's novel "The Mandarins," which featured a thinly disguised account of her affair with Algren. De Beauvoir is mentioned so frequently in the essays that accompany this edition, was a powerful and important writer in her own right, and served as a witness to the setting and characters of this novel (at Algren's invitation). She herself fell in love with this side of Chicago when she visited the city -- or maybe, she only fell in love with Algren, but out of love for him, extended that love to the city that made him. In any case, this edition screams for her voice and perception of Algren alongside that of the usual suspects of Royko, Terkel, and Vonnegut.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17385349554446515192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_2404372086'><a id="like_count_review_2404372086" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/2404372086?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F383022.The_Man_With_the_Golden_Arm">Like</a></span>

<div id="review_2919026105" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2919026105" />
    <a title="Susan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41945443-susan"><img alt="Susan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590870238p2/41945443.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2919026105?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Susan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Susan" href="/user/show/41945443-susan">Susan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/8687346-the-man-with-the-golden-arm">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/41945443-susan?shelf=owned-books">owned-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2919026105" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9714538948722364919">There's been a lot of talk about Algren lately due to a new biography, so I thought I'd finally get around to reading this, since it's been on my shelf forever. I guess there were a lot of guys writing these gritty urban stories after they came back from WWII.<br/><br/>It took me about 50 pages to get into the rhythm of his prose -- so much mook-speak. And so many extended acrimonious conversations going nowhere. I felt sorriest for Frankie's wife, but I could see how she goaded him into wanting to leave </span>
  <span id="freeText9714538948722364919" style="display:none">There's been a lot of talk about Algren lately due to a new biography, so I thought I'd finally get around to reading this, since it's been on my shelf forever. I guess there were a lot of guys writing these gritty urban stories after they came back from WWII.<br /><br />It took me about 50 pages to get into the rhythm of his prose -- so much mook-speak. And so many extended acrimonious conversations going nowhere. I felt sorriest for Frankie's wife, but I could see how she goaded him into wanting to leave her. Also very frustrating to see Frankie's inability to stay off the drugs after repeatedly getting clean, although that was probably a foregone conclusion. He was a truly conflicted character. A pretty sad book over all.<br /><br />I watched the movie again after finishing and boy did Hollywood tamper with the book. (Why am I surprised?) I'd have been furious if I were Algren.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9714538948722364919" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_369479724" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/369479724" />
    <a title="Sophie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1073899-sophie"><img alt="Sophie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1403647332p2/1073899.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/369479724?book_show_action=true">Jul 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sophie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sophie" href="/user/show/1073899-sophie">Sophie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1073899-sophie?shelf=chicago">chicago</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer369479724" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11804769476388358250">"Blackmail and terrorism, incest and pauperism, embezzlement and horse theft, tampering and procuring, abduction and quackery, adultery and mackery." <br/><br/>"That was the way things were because that was how things had always been. Which was why they could never be any different." <br/><br/>"'He's like me,' Frankie explained, 'never drinks. Unless he's alone or with somebody.'"<br/><br/>"Faces bloody as raw pork ground slowly in the great city's grinder; faces like burst white bags, one with eyes like some dying hen's and</span>
  <span id="freeText11804769476388358250" style="display:none">"Blackmail and terrorism, incest and pauperism, embezzlement and horse theft, tampering and procuring, abduction and quackery, adultery and mackery." <br /><br />"That was the way things were because that was how things had always been. Which was why they could never be any different." <br /><br />"'He's like me,' Frankie explained, 'never drinks. Unless he's alone or with somebody.'"<br /><br />"Faces bloody as raw pork ground slowly in the great city's grinder; faces like burst white bags, one with eyes like some dying hen's and one as bold as a cornered bulldog's; eyes with the small bright gleam of hysteria and eyes curtained by the dull half glaze of grief."<br /><br />"they felt they had merely emerged from the wrong side of its billboards."<br /><br />"Johnny had till now tried nothing but whisky. A process which left him feeling like somebody new every day."<br /><br />"He was many men and no man at all. He was a hysterical little bundle of possibilities that could never come true."<br /><br />"QUIET Or Out You Go Too"<br /><br />"Why would anyone want to eat peanuts in the dark with the door exactly two inches ajar?"<br /><br />"A dread she sometimes evaded by reaching for an outsized album labeled, in her own childish and belabored hand, <i> My Scrapbook of Fatal Accidence. </i> "<br /><br />"Nobody heard but Owner. And Owner, in his clean-shaven, bald and bespectacled indifference, cared not a bartender's button."<br /><br />"'They don't <i> have </i> to see me,' he assured the black bar mirror of his mind with that smug and buttery smile, 'they could just <i> thmell </i> me.'"<br /><br />"What's your habit, Jack the Rabbit?"<br /><br />"He whistled slyly to himself, seeing, over Nifty Louie's shoulder, a slow and stiff burlesque moving down the curtained runway of his mind: an endless all-night carnival playing for blind Piggy-O alone. As it had played, off and on, since he had last had eyes." <br /><br />"She remembered the years of their courtship like remembering and alien land."<br /><br />"Years when everything was so well arranged."<br /><br />"There's people in hell want ice water too."<br /><br />"He looked like a man who had never seen a cloud."<br /><br />"'Lies are just a poor man's pennies,' Violet told her."<br /><br />"'You leave me agasted,' Sophie told her, knotting her babushka under her chin with impatience in every fingertip, 'I just don't see how some of you Division Street women live, that's <i> all </i>.'"<br /><br />"A wind began parting them, like a curtain parting upon the opening act of a play staged just for her, to reveal a paper moon pasted stiffly -- for as long as paste might hold -- but one that did not weave with light as the real moon was supposed to do." <br /><br />"The city too seemed a little insane."<br /><br />"She could tell just by the way once familiar doorways had come to look menacing in the morning light, ready to be slammed in the face of anyone who knocked at all. Nobody was at home to anyone else any more."<br /><br />"One wind or another"<br /><br />"You can weave gold where there is no gold."<br /><br />"But Sunday morning was always pretty rugged for anything but sleep. All the miracles were performed on Saturday night, it seemed."<br /><br />"'Where'd that motherless animal go?' she wanted to know. Just like that: 'Where's that motherless animal hiding?'"<br /><br />"Then the upstairs door closed behind Louie; and none had heard the door downstairs open at all."<br /><br />"He wasn't as certain, as he once had been, that Violet was an unmixed blessing."<br /><br />"At the bar there was such a crush that the liquor ran out three times and emergency rations had to be rushed in by a squad of four flying lushes. It was one of those nights when everyone felt, for some reason, he really never had to go to work again at all."<br /><br />"He who drinks with grace is ever welcome in this place but he who drinks and starts to swear is never welcome anywhere."<br /><br />"He caught a glimpse of a butcher holding a broken-necked rooster, both butcher and rooster sliding one limp dark eye sidewise at him as he passed."<br /><br />"Trueblue pals"<br /><br />"lush workers and mush workers"<br /><br />"<i> Have Doc Bunson call for my body personlly,...He is a personl friend of mine and no autopsy in necessry </i>"<br /><br />"Girls who would and girls who wouldn't. If they did they were no good and if they didn't what good were they?"<br /><br />"This place gives me the baloney blues."<br /><br />"'Rubber heels 'n fisheyes again' was the word on the meatloaf and tapioca"<br /><br />"I'd feel bad if you'd kissed too many but I'd feel worse if you hadn't kissed any."<br /><br />"These were days of clouds swollen gray with promise of rain -- only to burst emptily and reveal the deepest sort of blue drifting there all the time."<br /><br />"As the fever lowered Frankie dreamed of someone folding and refolding bundles of newspapers right beside his cot and forced himself awake to see who it was this time. Only the old woman of the wind, there on the other side of the pane, wrapping the great sheets of the rain."<br /><br />"A loneliness that took on substance and form, like a crouching man wearing some sort of faded, outworn uniform."<br /><br />"Sparrow watched it flutter up there with the first rumors of evening, and his own heart pinked with the wind."<br /><br />"It had been so long since it had been cleaned that, even on summer noons with the sun like a brass bell across pavement and rooftop and wall, the light sifted down here with a chill autumnal hue."<br /><br />"My little red wagon is hitched to yours, DeWitt."<br /><br />"Move fast, feet. Jump off."<br /><br />"Counting out the weather in all the streets of evening with no true hope for the bright alarm of morning any more."<br /><br />"I want the frim fram sauce."<br /><br />"Red sails in the sunset, All day I've been blue."<br /><br />"In the uproar and the odor..."<br /><br />"I'm just gonna look around, the places where the people are,' he reported over his shoulder."<br /><br />"And the eyes so dark and buttoned so tightly. So pinched by that private, midnight-colored grief."<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11804769476388358250" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('svK9QbUBukpuBVyLbF+hZnPxDDyB3nnwT4hk5avEUK+hoFMt+5ce3RguSD0/EYHHMrxjVX3UY5m+x9O6Mfq0vg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('8yhFAU1UqP2j2FufpaR+UkvoPAth5bgIxOtFIh30U8DgeqttA8IMatXzTyn26l7zCqVTYp3vomE1pPJ9h8q30Q==')})
  }

The men have maintained long-distance friendship in a postwar loneliness that haunts them both, and which has swallowed Exley whole. Precocious, brilliant, sensitive, at home in the books they read together, these two have been, in Leith\'s words, delivered by literature.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/17220.Nelson_Algren">About Nelson Algren</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/17220.Nelson_Algren"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1201393176p3/17220.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/17220.Nelson_Algren">Nelson Algren
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
227
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer3324949295638867894">Born of Swedish-immigrant parents, Nelson Ahlgren Abraham moved at an early age from Detroit to Chicago. At Illinois University he studied journalism. His experiences as a migrant worker during the Depression provided the material for his first novel <i>Somebody in Boots</i> (1935). Throughout his life Algren identified with the American underdog. From 1936 to 1940 (the highpoint of left-wing ideas on th</span>
  <span id="freeText3324949295638867894" style="display:none">Born of Swedish-immigrant parents, Nelson Ahlgren Abraham moved at an early age from Detroit to Chicago. At Illinois University he studied journalism. His experiences as a migrant worker during the Depression provided the material for his first novel <i>Somebody in Boots</i> (1935). Throughout his life Algren identified with the American underdog. From 1936 to 1940 (the highpoint of left-wing ideas on the US literary scene) he was editor of the Illinois Writers' Project. In 1942, after putting the finishing touches to his second novel, he joined the war as an enlisted man. <i>Never Come Morning</i> received universal acclaim, and eventually sold over a million copies.<br /><br />Algren's style is a dark naturalism that passionately records the details of trapped urban existence with flashes of melancholy poetry. His characters are the lowlife drifters, whores, junkies and barflies of poverty row. He records the bravado of their colloquial language and lays their predicament bare.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3324949295638867894" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>
